</section><section data-mw-section-id="6" id="mwAcU"><h3 id="The_position_of_turtles">The position of turtles</h3>
<p id="mwAcY">The placement of turtles has historically been highly variable. Classically, turtles were considered to be related to the primitive anapsid reptiles.<sup about="#mwt268" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Romer, A 1977"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwAcc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcg">[29]</span></a></sup> Molecular work has usually placed turtles within the diapsids. As of 2013, three turtle genomes have been sequenced.<sup about="#mwt272" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2013_31-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Gilbert2013"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Gilbert2013-31"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Gilbert2013-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwAck"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAco">[30]</span></a></sup> The results place turtles as a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sister_clade" title="Sister clade" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAcs">sister clade</a> to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archosaur" title="Archosaur" id="mwAcw">archosaurs</a>, the group that includes crocodiles, dinosaurs, and birds.<sup about="#mwt276" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-32" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-32"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAc0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAc4">[31]</span></a></sup> However, in their comparative analysis of the timing of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Organogenesis" title="Organogenesis" id="mwAc8">organogenesis</a>, Werneburg and Sánchez-Villagra (2009) found support for the hypothesis that turtles belong to a separate clade within <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauropsida" title="Sauropsida" id="mwAdA">Sauropsida</a>, outside the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauria" title="Sauria" id="mwAdE">saurian</a> clade altogether.<sup about="#mwt280" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Werneburg_and_Sánchez-Villagra_2009_33-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Werneburg and Sánchez-Villagra 2009"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Werneburg_and_Sánchez-Villagra_2009-33"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Werneburg_and_Sánchez-Villagra_2009-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwAdI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAdM">[32]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="7" id="mwAdQ"><h2 id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt281" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Evolution of reptiles"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAdU"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt281" id="mwAdY">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Evolution_of_reptiles" title="Evolution of reptiles">Evolution of reptiles</a></div>

<section data-mw-section-id="8" id="mwAdc"><h3 id="Origin_of_the_reptiles">Origin of the reptiles</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAdg"><a href="./File:Hylonomus_BW.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAdk"><img resource="./File:Hylonomus_BW.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/220px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="338" data-file-type="bitmap" height="93" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/330px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Hylonomus_BW.jpg/440px-Hylonomus_BW.jpg 2x" id="mwAdo"/></a><figcaption id="mwAds">An early reptile <i id="mwAdw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hylonomus" title="Hylonomus" id="mwAd0">Hylonomus</a></i></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAd4"><a href="./File:Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAd8"><img resource="./File:Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/220px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1932" data-file-type="bitmap" height="165" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/330px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg/440px-Europasaurus_holgeri_Scene_2.jpg 2x" id="mwAeA"/></a><figcaption id="mwAeE">Mesozoic scene showing typical reptilian megafauna: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwAeI">dinosaurs</a> including <i id="mwAeM"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Europasaurus_holgeri" title="Europasaurus holgeri" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAeQ">Europasaurus holgeri</a></i>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iguanodont" title="Iguanodont" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAeU">iguanodonts</a>, and <i id="mwAeY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archaeopteryx_lithographica" title="Archaeopteryx lithographica" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAec">Archaeopteryx lithographica</a></i> perched on the foreground tree stump</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAeg">The origin of the reptiles lies about 310–320 million years ago, in the steaming swamps of the late <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous" id="mwAek">Carboniferous</a> period, when the first reptiles evolved from advanced <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha" id="mwAeo">reptiliomorphs</a>.<sup about="#mwt288" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Laurin_95_34-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Laurin 95"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Laurin_95-34"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Laurin_95-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAes"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAew">[33]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAe0">The oldest known animal that may have been an <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amniote" title="Amniote" id="mwAe4">amniote</a> is <i id="mwAe8"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Casineria" title="Casineria" id="mwAfA">Casineria</a></i> (though it may have been a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Temnospondyli" title="Temnospondyli" id="mwAfE">temnospondyl</a>).<sup about="#mwt298" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-35" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-35"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-35" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mwAfI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfM">[34]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt302" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-36" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-36"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 35;" id="mwAfQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfU">[35]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt306" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-37" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-37"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwAfY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfc">[36]</span></a></sup> A series of footprints from the fossil strata of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia" id="mwAfg">Nova Scotia</a> dated to <span class="nowrap" about="#mwt294" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"val","href":"./Template:Val"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"315"},"ul":{"wt":"Ma"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAfk"><span data-sort-value="7015994064400000000♠"></span>315<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Megaannum" title="Megaannum" class="mw-redirect">Ma</a></span> show typical reptilian toes and imprints of scales.<sup about="#mwt310" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-38" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-38"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mwAfo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAfs">[37]</span></a></sup> These tracks are attributed to <i id="mwAfw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hylonomus" title="Hylonomus" id="mwAf0">Hylonomus</a></i>, the oldest unquestionable reptile known.<sup about="#mwt314" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-39" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-39"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAf4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAf8">[38]</span></a></sup>
It was a small, lizard-like animal, about <span about="#mwt317" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"convert","href":"./Template:Convert"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"20"},"2":{"wt":"to"},"3":{"wt":"30"},"4":{"wt":"cm"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAgA">20 to 30 centimetres (7.9 to 11.8</span><span typeof="mw:Entity" about="#mwt317"> </span><span about="#mwt317">in)</span> long, with numerous sharp teeth indicating an insectivorous diet.<sup about="#mwt321" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-EoDP_40-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"EoDP"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-EoDP-40"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-EoDP-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 39;" id="mwAgE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgI">[39]</span></a></sup> Other examples include <i id="mwAgM"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Westlothiana" title="Westlothiana" id="mwAgQ">Westlothiana</a></i> (for the moment considered a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha" id="mwAgU">reptiliomorph</a> rather than a true <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amniote" title="Amniote" id="mwAgY">amniote</a>)<sup about="#mwt325" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-41" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-41"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-41" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mwAgc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgg">[40]</span></a></sup> and <i id="mwAgk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paleothyris" title="Paleothyris" id="mwAgo">Paleothyris</a></i>, both of similar build and presumably similar habit.</p>

<p id="mwAgs">However, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Microsaur" title="Microsaur" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAgw">microsaurs</a> have been at times considered true reptiles, so an earlier origin is possible.<sup about="#mwt330" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_42-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-auto-42"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-auto-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mwAg0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAg4">[41]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="9" id="mwAg8"><h3 id="Rise_of_the_reptiles">Rise of the reptiles</h3>
<p id="mwAhA">The earliest amniotes, including stem-reptiles (those amniotes closer to modern reptiles than to mammals), were largely overshadowed by larger stem-tetrapods, such as <i id="mwAhE"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cochleosaurus" title="Cochleosaurus" id="mwAhI">Cochleosaurus</a></i>, and remained a small, inconspicuous part of the fauna until the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carboniferous_Rainforest_Collapse" title="Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAhM">Carboniferous Rainforest Collapse</a>.<sup about="#mwt338" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_43-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SahneyBentonFalconLang 2010RainforestCollapse"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-43"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAhQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhU">[42]</span></a></sup> This sudden collapse affected several large groups. Primitive tetrapods were particularly devastated, while stem-reptiles fared better, being ecologically adapted to the drier conditions that followed. Primitive tetrapods, like modern amphibians, need to return to water to lay eggs; in contrast, amniotes, like modern reptiles<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAhY"> </span>– whose eggs possess a shell that allows them to be laid on land<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAhc"> </span>– were better adapted to the new conditions. Amniotes acquired new niches at a faster rate than before the collapse and at a much faster rate than primitive tetrapods. They acquired new feeding strategies including herbivory and carnivory, previously only having been insectivores and piscivores.<sup about="#mwt339" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse_43-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SahneyBentonFalconLang 2010RainforestCollapse"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-SahneyBentonFalconLang_2010RainforestCollapse-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mwAhg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhk">[42]</span></a></sup> From this point forward, reptiles dominated communities and had a greater diversity than primitive tetrapods, setting the stage for the Mesozoic (known as the Age of Reptiles).<sup about="#mwt343" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010_44-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010-44"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwAho"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhs">[43]</span></a></sup> One of the best known early stem-reptiles is <i id="mwAhw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesosaurus" title="Mesosaurus" id="mwAh0">Mesosaurus</a></i>, a genus from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cisuralian" title="Cisuralian" id="mwAh4">Early Permian</a> that had returned to water, feeding on fish.</p>

<p id="mwAh8">A 2021 examination of reptile diversity in the Carboniferous and Permian suggests a much higher degree of diversity than previously thought, comparable or even exceeding that of synapsids. Thus, the "First Age of Reptiles" was proposed.<sup about="#mwt345" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_42-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-auto-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mwAiA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAiE">[41]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="10" id="mwAiI"><h3 id="Anapsids,_synapsids,_diapsids,_and_sauropsids"><span id="Anapsids.2C_synapsids.2C_diapsids.2C_and_sauropsids" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>Anapsids, synapsids, diapsids, and sauropsids</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAiM"><a href="./File:Skull_comparison.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAiQ"><img resource="./File:Skull_comparison.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/170px-Skull_comparison.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="568" data-file-height="864" data-file-type="bitmap" height="259" width="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/255px-Skull_comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Skull_comparison.png/340px-Skull_comparison.png 2x" id="mwAiU"/></a><figcaption id="mwAiY"><b id="mwAic">A</b><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAig"> </span>=<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAik"> </span>Anapsid,<br id="mwAio"/> <b id="mwAis">B</b><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAiw"> </span>=<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAi0"> </span>Synapsid,<br id="mwAi4"/> <b id="mwAi8">C</b><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAjA"> </span>=<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAjE"> </span>Diapsid</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAjI">It was traditionally assumed that the first reptiles retained an <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anapsid" title="Anapsid" id="mwAjM">anapsid</a> skull inherited from their ancestors.<sup about="#mwt359" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Coven_45-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Coven"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Coven-45"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Coven-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAjQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAjU">[44]</span></a></sup> This type of skull has a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skull_roof" title="Skull roof" id="mwAjY">skull roof</a> with only holes for the nostrils, eyes and a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pineal_eye" title="Pineal eye" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAjc">pineal eye</a>.<sup about="#mwt363" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Romer, A 1977"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwAjg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAjk">[29]</span></a></sup> The discoveries of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Synapsid" title="Synapsid" id="mwAjo">synapsid</a>-like openings (see below) in the skull roof of the skulls of several members of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parareptilia" title="Parareptilia" id="mwAjs">Parareptilia</a> (the clade containing most of the amniotes traditionally referred to as "anapsids"), including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lanthanosuchoidea" title="Lanthanosuchoidea" id="mwAjw">lanthanosuchoids</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Millerettidae" title="Millerettidae" id="mwAj0">millerettids</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bolosauridae" title="Bolosauridae" id="mwAj4">bolosaurids</a>, some <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nycteroleteridae" title="Nycteroleteridae" id="mwAj8">nycteroleterids</a>, some <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Procolophonoidea" title="Procolophonoidea" id="mwAkA">procolophonoids</a> and at least some <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesosaur" title="Mesosaur" id="mwAkE">mesosaurs</a><sup about="#mwt367" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-46" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-46"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-46" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 45;" id="mwAkI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkM">[45]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt371" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-TsujiMuller2009FR_47-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"TsujiMuller2009FR"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-TsujiMuller2009FR-47"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-TsujiMuller2009FR-47" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 46;" id="mwAkQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkU">[46]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt375" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-PineiroetalCRP2012_48-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"PineiroetalCRP2012"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-PineiroetalCRP2012-48"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-PineiroetalCRP2012-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwAkY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkc">[47]</span></a></sup> made it more ambiguous and it's currently uncertain whether the ancestral amniote had an anapsid-like or synapsid-like skull.<sup about="#mwt376" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-PineiroetalCRP2012_48-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"PineiroetalCRP2012"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-PineiroetalCRP2012-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwAkg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkk">[47]</span></a></sup> These animals are traditionally referred to as "anapsids", and form a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paraphyly" title="Paraphyly" id="mwAko">paraphyletic</a> basic stock from which other groups evolved.<sup about="#mwt377" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-modestoanderson2004_20-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"modestoanderson2004"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-modestoanderson2004-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwAks"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkw">[19]</span></a></sup> Very shortly after the first amniotes appeared, a lineage called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Synapsida" title="Synapsida" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAk0">Synapsida</a> split off; this group was characterized by a temporal opening in the skull behind each eye to give room for the jaw muscle to move. These are the "mammal-like amniotes", or stem-mammals, that later gave rise to the true <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammals" title="Mammals" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAk4">mammals</a>.<sup about="#mwt381" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-49" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-49"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwAk8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlA">[48]</span></a></sup> Soon after, another group evolved a similar trait, this time with a double opening behind each eye, earning them the name <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diapsida" title="Diapsida" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAlE">Diapsida</a> ("two arches").<sup about="#mwt382" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Coven_45-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Coven"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Coven-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAlI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlM">[44]</span></a></sup> The function of the holes in these groups was to lighten the skull and give room for the jaw muscles to move, allowing for a more powerful bite.<sup about="#mwt383" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Romer, A 1977"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwAlQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlU">[29]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAlY">Turtles have been traditionally believed to be surviving parareptiles, on the basis of their anapsid skull structure, which was assumed to be primitive trait.<sup about="#mwt397" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-50" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-50"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-50" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 49;" id="mwAlc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlg">[49]</span></a></sup> The rationale for this classification has been disputed, with some arguing that turtles are diapsids that evolved anapsid skulls in order to improve their armor.<sup about="#mwt398" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Laurin_95_34-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Laurin 95"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Laurin_95-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwAlk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlo">[33]</span></a></sup> Later morphological <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics" id="mwAls">phylogenetic</a> studies with this in mind placed turtles firmly within Diapsida.<sup about="#mwt402" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Rieppel_51-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Rieppel"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Rieppel-51"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Rieppel-51" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 50;" id="mwAlw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAl0">[50]</span></a></sup> All <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics" id="mwAl4">molecular</a> studies have strongly upheld the placement of turtles within diapsids, most commonly as a sister group to extant <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archosaur" title="Archosaur" id="mwAl8">archosaurs</a>.<sup about="#mwt406" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Zardoya_25-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Zardoya"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Zardoya-25"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Zardoya-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwAmA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAmE">[24]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt410" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Iwabe_26-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Iwabe"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Iwabe-26"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Iwabe-26" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mwAmI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAmM">[25]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt414" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Roos_27-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Roos"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Roos-27"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Roos-27" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 26;" id="mwAmQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAmU">[26]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt418" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Katsu_28-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Katsu"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Katsu-28"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Katsu-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 27;" id="mwAmY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAmc">[27]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="11" id="mwAmg"><h3 id="Permian_reptiles">Permian reptiles</h3>
<p id="mwAmk">With the close of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carboniferous" title="Carboniferous" id="mwAmo">Carboniferous</a>, the amniotes became the dominant tetrapod fauna. While primitive, terrestrial <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptiliomorpha" title="Reptiliomorpha" id="mwAms">reptiliomorphs</a> still existed, the synapsid amniotes evolved the first truly terrestrial <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Megafauna" title="Megafauna" id="mwAmw">megafauna</a> (giant animals) in the form of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pelycosaurs" title="Pelycosaurs" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAm0">pelycosaurs</a>, such as <i id="mwAm4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Edaphosaurus" title="Edaphosaurus" id="mwAm8">Edaphosaurus</a></i> and the carnivorous <i id="mwAnA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon" id="mwAnE">Dimetrodon</a></i>. In the mid-Permian period, the climate became drier, resulting in a change of fauna: The pelycosaurs were replaced by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Therapsids" title="Therapsids" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAnI">therapsids</a>.<sup about="#mwt423" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2001_52-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"autogenerated2001"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-autogenerated2001-52"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-autogenerated2001-52" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 51;" id="mwAnM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAnQ">[51]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAnU">The parareptiles, whose massive <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skull_roof" title="Skull roof" id="mwAnY">skull roofs</a> had no postorbital holes, continued and flourished throughout the Permian. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pareiasaur" title="Pareiasaur" id="mwAnc">pareiasaurian</a> parareptiles reached giant proportions in the late Permian, eventually disappearing at the close of the period (the turtles being possible survivors).<sup about="#mwt425" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2001_52-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"autogenerated2001"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-autogenerated2001-52" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 51;" id="mwAng"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAnk">[51]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAno">Early in the period, the modern reptiles, or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauria" title="Sauria" id="mwAns">crown-group reptiles</a>, evolved and split into two main lineages: the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha" id="mwAnw">Archosauromorpha</a> (forebears of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle" title="Turtle" id="mwAn0">turtles</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodile" title="Crocodile" id="mwAn4">crocodiles</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwAn8">dinosaurs</a>) and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lepidosauromorpha" title="Lepidosauromorpha" id="mwAoA">Lepidosauromorpha</a> (predecessors of modern <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lizard" title="Lizard" id="mwAoE">lizards</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuatara" title="Tuatara" id="mwAoI">tuataras</a>). Both groups remained lizard-like and relatively small and inconspicuous during the Permian.</p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="12" id="mwAoM"><h3 id="Mesozoic_reptiles">Mesozoic reptiles</h3>
<p id="mwAoQ">The close of the Permian saw the greatest mass extinction known (see the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Permian–Triassic_extinction_event" title="Permian–Triassic extinction event" id="mwAoU">Permian–Triassic extinction event</a>), an event prolonged by the combination of two or more distinct extinction pulses.<sup about="#mwt432" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_53-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-53"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 52;" id="mwAoY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAoc">[52]</span></a></sup> Most of the earlier parareptile and synapsid megafauna disappeared, being replaced by the true reptiles, particularly <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archosauromorpha" title="Archosauromorpha" id="mwAog">archosauromorphs</a>. These were characterized by elongated hind legs and an erect pose, the early forms looking somewhat like long-legged crocodiles. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Archosaur" title="Archosaur" id="mwAok">archosaurs</a> became the dominant group during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Triassic" title="Triassic" id="mwAoo">Triassic</a> period, though it took 30 million years before their diversity was as great as the animals that lived in the Permian.<sup about="#mwt433" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction_53-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-SahneyBenton2008RecoveryFromProfoundExtinction-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 52;" id="mwAos"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAow">[52]</span></a></sup> Archosaurs developed into the well-known <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwAo0">dinosaurs</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur" id="mwAo4">pterosaurs</a>, as well as the ancestors of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodile" title="Crocodile" id="mwAo8">crocodiles</a>. Since reptiles, first <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rauisuchia" title="Rauisuchia" id="mwApA">rauisuchians</a> and then dinosaurs, dominated the Mesozoic era, the interval is popularly known as the "Age of Reptiles". The dinosaurs also developed smaller forms, including the feather-bearing smaller <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Theropoda" title="Theropoda" id="mwApE">theropods</a>. In the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous" id="mwApI">Cretaceous</a> period, these gave rise to the first true <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Birds" title="Birds" class="mw-redirect" id="mwApM">birds</a>.<sup about="#mwt437" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-divergence_54-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"divergence"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-divergence-54"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-divergence-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwApQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwApU">[53]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwApY">The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sister_group" title="Sister group" id="mwApc">sister group</a> to Archosauromorpha is <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lepidosauromorpha" title="Lepidosauromorpha" id="mwApg">Lepidosauromorpha</a>, containing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lizard" title="Lizard" id="mwApk">lizards</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tuatara" title="Tuatara" id="mwApo">tuataras</a>, as well as their fossil relatives. Lepidosauromorpha contained at least one major group of the Mesozoic sea reptiles: the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mosasaurs" title="Mosasaurs" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAps">mosasaurs</a>, which lived during the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous" id="mwApw">Cretaceous</a> period. The phylogenetic placement of other main groups of fossil sea reptiles – the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyopterygia" title="Ichthyopterygia" id="mwAp0">ichthyopterygians</a> (including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur" id="mwAp4">ichthyosaurs</a>) and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia" id="mwAp8">sauropterygians</a>, which evolved in the early Triassic – is more controversial. Different authors linked these groups either to lepidosauromorphs<sup about="#mwt446" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Gauthier-1994-Prothero-Schoch_1-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Gauthier-1994-Prothero-Schoch"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Gauthier-1994-Prothero-Schoch-1"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Gauthier-1994-Prothero-Schoch-1" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 1;" id="mwAqA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqE">[1]</span></a></sup> or to archosauromorphs,<sup about="#mwt450" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-55" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-55"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-55" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 54;" id="mwAqI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqM">[54]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt454" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-56" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-56"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-56" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 55;" id="mwAqQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqU">[55]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt458" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-57" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-57"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-57" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 56;" id="mwAqY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqc">[56]</span></a></sup> and ichthyopterygians were also argued to be diapsids that did not belong to the least inclusive clade containing lepidosauromorphs and archosauromorphs.<sup about="#mwt462" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-58" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-58"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-58" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 57;" id="mwAqg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqk">[57]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="13" id="mwAqo"><h3 id="Cenozoic_reptiles">Cenozoic reptiles</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAqs"><a href="./File:Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAqw"><img resource="./File:Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/220px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="567" data-file-height="463" data-file-type="bitmap" height="180" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/330px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg/440px-Varanus_priscus_Melbourne_Museum.jpg 2x" id="mwAq0"/></a><figcaption id="mwAq4"><i id="mwAq8"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Varanus_priscus" title="Varanus priscus" class="mw-redirect" id="mwArA">Varanus priscus</a></i> was a giant carnivorous <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Goanna" title="Goanna" id="mwArE">goanna</a> lizard, perhaps as long as 7<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwArI"> </span>metres and weighing up to 1,940<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwArM"> </span>kilograms<sup about="#mwt467" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-59" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-59"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-59" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 58;" id="mwArQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwArU">[58]</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwArY"><a href="./File:Champsosaurus_natator.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwArc"><img resource="./File:Champsosaurus_natator.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/220px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="4196" data-file-height="1591" data-file-type="bitmap" height="83" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/330px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Champsosaurus_natator.jpg/440px-Champsosaurus_natator.jpg 2x" id="mwArg"/></a><figcaption id="mwArk">Skeleton of <i id="mwAro"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Champsosaurus" title="Champsosaurus" id="mwArs">Champsosaurus</a></i>, a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Choristodere" title="Choristodere" class="mw-redirect" id="mwArw">choristodere</a>, the latest surviving order of extinct reptiles. The last known choristoderes are known from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Miocene" title="Miocene" id="mwAr0">Miocene</a>, around 11.3 million years ago </figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwAr4">The close of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous" id="mwAr8">Cretaceous</a> period saw the demise of the Mesozoic era reptilian megafauna (see the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cretaceous–Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event" id="mwAsA">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>, also known as K-T<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAsE"> </span>extinction event). Of the large marine reptiles, only <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle" id="mwAsI">sea turtles</a> were left; and of the non-marine large reptiles, only the semi-aquatic <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia" id="mwAsM">crocodiles</a> and broadly similar <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Choristodera" title="Choristodera" id="mwAsQ">choristoderes</a> survived the extinction, with last members of the latter, the lizard-like <i id="mwAsU"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lazarussuchus" title="Lazarussuchus" id="mwAsY">Lazarussuchus</a></i>, becoming extinct in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Miocene" title="Miocene" id="mwAsc">Miocene</a>.<sup about="#mwt475" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-60" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-60"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-60" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 59;" id="mwAsg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsk">[59]</span></a></sup> Of the great host of dinosaurs dominating the Mesozoic, only the small beaked <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Birds" title="Birds" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAso">birds</a> survived. This dramatic extinction pattern at the end of the Mesozoic led into the Cenozoic. Mammals and birds filled the empty niches left behind by the reptilian megafauna and, while reptile diversification slowed, bird and mammal diversification took an exponential turn.<sup about="#mwt476" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010_44-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwAss"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsw">[43]</span></a></sup> However, reptiles were still important components of the megafauna, particularly in the form of large and giant <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tortoise" title="Tortoise" id="mwAs0">tortoises</a>.<sup about="#mwt480" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_61-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Hansen"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Hansen-61"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Hansen-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 60;" id="mwAs4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAs8">[60]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt484" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Cione_62-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Cione"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Cione-62"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Cione-62" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwAtA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtE">[61]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAtI">After the extinction of most archosaur and marine reptile lines by the end of the Cretaceous, reptile diversification continued throughout the Cenozoic. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwAtM">Squamates</a> took a massive hit during the K–Pg event, only recovering ten million years after it,<sup about="#mwt492" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-LBG12_63-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"LBG12"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-LBG12-63"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-LBG12-63" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 62;" id="mwAtQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtU">[62]</span></a></sup> but they underwent a great radiation event once they recovered, and today squamates make up the majority of living reptiles (> 95%).<sup about="#mwt496" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-The_Reptile_Database_64-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"The Reptile Database"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-The_Reptile_Database-64"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-The_Reptile_Database-64" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 63;" id="mwAtY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtc">[63]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt501" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-65" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-65"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 64;" id="mwAtg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtk">[64]</span></a></sup> Approximately 10,000 extant species of traditional reptiles are known, with birds adding about 10,000 more, almost twice the number of mammals, represented by about 5,700<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAto"> </span>living species (excluding <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals" id="mwAts">domesticated</a> species).<sup about="#mwt505" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-66" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-66"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-66" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwAtw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAt0">[65]</span></a></sup></p>

<table class="wikitable" id="mwAt4">

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="14" id="mwAxA"><h2 id="Morphology_and_physiology">Morphology and physiology<span class="anchor" id="Systems" about="#mwt511" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"anchor","href":"./Template:Anchor"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Systems"}},"i":0}}]}'></span></h2>

<section data-mw-section-id="15" id="mwAxE"><h3 id="Circulation">Circulation</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAxI"><a href="./File:Wiki_varano.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAxM"><img resource="./File:Wiki_varano.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg/220px-Wiki_varano.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="404" data-file-height="240" data-file-type="bitmap" height="131" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg/330px-Wiki_varano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Wiki_varano.jpg 2x" id="mwAxQ"/></a><figcaption id="mwAxU">Thermographic image of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard" id="mwAxY">monitor lizards</a></figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwAxc">All <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lepidosauria" title="Lepidosauria" id="mwAxg">lepidosaurs</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle" title="Turtle" id="mwAxk">turtles</a> have a three-chambered <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Heart" title="Heart" id="mwAxo">heart</a> consisting of two <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)" id="mwAxs">atria</a>, one variably partitioned <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)" id="mwAxw">ventricle</a>, and two aortas that lead to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Systemic_circulation" title="Systemic circulation" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAx0">systemic circulation</a>. The degree of mixing of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oxygen" title="Oxygen" id="mwAx4">oxygenated</a> and deoxygenated blood in the three-chambered heart varies depending on the species and physiological state. Under different conditions, deoxygenated blood can be shunted back to the body or oxygenated blood can be shunted back to the lungs. This variation in blood flow has been hypothesized to allow more effective thermoregulation and longer diving times for aquatic species, but has not been shown to be a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fitness_(biology)" title="Fitness (biology)" id="mwAx8">fitness</a> advantage.<sup about="#mwt516" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-68" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-68"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-68" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwAyA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAyE">[67]</span></a></sup></p>

<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAyI"><a href="./File:Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAyM"><img resource="./File:Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/220px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="748" data-file-height="838" data-file-type="bitmap" height="246" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/330px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png/440px-Bisected_Iguana_Heart_Image.png 2x" id="mwAyQ"/></a><figcaption id="mwAyU">Juvenile <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iguana" title="Iguana" id="mwAyY">Iguana</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Heart" title="Heart" id="mwAyc">heart</a> bisected through the ventricle, bisecting the left and right atrium</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwAyg">For example,<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAyk"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iguana" title="Iguana" id="mwAyo">Iguana</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAys"> </span>hearts, like the majority of the<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAyw"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwAy0">squamates</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAy4"> </span>hearts, are composed of three chambers with two aorta and one ventricle, cardiac involuntary muscles.<sup about="#mwt522" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-69" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-69"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-69" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 68;" id="mwAy8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzA">[68]</span></a></sup> The main structures of the heart are the<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzE"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sinus_venosus" title="Sinus venosus" id="mwAzI">sinus venosus</a>, the pacemaker, the<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzM"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)" id="mwAzQ">left atrium</a>, the<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzU"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Atrium_(heart)" title="Atrium (heart)" id="mwAzY">right atrium</a>, the<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzc"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Heart_valve" title="Heart valve" id="mwAzg">atrioventricular valve</a>, the cavum venosum, cavum arteriosum, the cavum pulmonale, the muscular ridge, the ventricular ridge,<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzk"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pulmonary_vein" title="Pulmonary vein" id="mwAzo">pulmonary veins</a>, and paired<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwAzs"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aortic_arches" title="Aortic arches" id="mwAzw">aortic arches</a>.<sup about="#mwt526" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-70" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-70"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-70" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 69;" id="mwAz0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAz4">[69]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwAz8">Some squamate species (e.g., pythons and monitor lizards) have three-chambered hearts that become functionally four-chambered hearts during contraction. This is made possible by a muscular ridge that subdivides the ventricle during <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cardiac_cycle" title="Cardiac cycle" id="mwA0A">ventricular diastole</a> and completely divides it during <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Systole_(medicine)" title="Systole (medicine)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA0E">ventricular systole</a>. Because of this ridge, some of these <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwA0I">squamates</a> are capable of producing ventricular pressure differentials that are equivalent to those seen in mammalian and avian hearts.<sup about="#mwt531" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-71" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-71"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-71" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 70;" id="mwA0M"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0Q">[70]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA0U"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia" id="mwA0Y">Crocodilians</a> have an anatomically four-chambered heart, similar to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bird" title="Bird" id="mwA0c">birds</a>, but also have two systemic aortas and are therefore capable of bypassing their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pulmonary_circulation" title="Pulmonary circulation" id="mwA0g">pulmonary circulation</a>.<sup about="#mwt536" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-72" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-72"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 71;" id="mwA0k"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0o">[71]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="16" id="mwA0s"><h3 id="Metabolism">Metabolism</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA0w"><a href="./File:Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA00"><img resource="./File:Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/220px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="700" data-file-type="bitmap" height="154" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/330px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png/440px-Homeothermy-poikilothermy.png 2x" id="mwA04"/></a><figcaption id="mwA08">Sustained energy output (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Joule" title="Joule" id="mwA1A">joules</a>) of a typical reptile versus a similar size mammal as a function of core body temperature. The mammal has a much higher peak output, but can only function over a very narrow range of body temperature.</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwA1E">Modern non-avian reptiles exhibit some form of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm" id="mwA1I">cold-bloodedness</a> (i.e. some mix of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm" id="mwA1M">poikilothermy</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ectotherm" title="Ectotherm" id="mwA1Q">ectothermy</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bradymetabolism" title="Bradymetabolism" id="mwA1U">bradymetabolism</a>) so that they have limited physiological means of keeping the body temperature constant and often rely on external sources of heat. Due to a less stable core temperature than <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bird" title="Bird" id="mwA1Y">birds</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammal" title="Mammal" id="mwA1c">mammals</a>, reptilian biochemistry requires <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Enzyme" title="Enzyme" id="mwA1g">enzymes</a> capable of maintaining efficiency over a greater range of temperatures than in the case for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded" id="mwA1k">warm-blooded</a> animals. The optimum body temperature range varies with species, but is typically below that of warm-blooded animals; for many lizards, it falls in the 24°–35<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1o"> </span>°C (75°–95<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA1s"> </span>°F) range,<sup about="#mwt539" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-73" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-73"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-73" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwA1w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA10">[72]</span></a></sup> while extreme heat-adapted species, like the American <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Desert_iguana" title="Desert iguana" id="mwA14">desert iguana</a> <i id="mwA18">Dipsosaurus dorsalis</i>, can have optimal physiological temperatures in the mammalian range, between 35° and 40<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA2A"> </span>°C (95° and 104<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA2E"> </span>°F).<sup about="#mwt540" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-74" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-74"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-74" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 73;" id="mwA2I"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA2M">[73]</span></a></sup> While the optimum temperature is often encountered when the animal is active, the low basal metabolism makes body temperature drop rapidly when the animal is inactive.</p>

<p id="mwA2Q">As in all animals, reptilian muscle action produces heat. In large reptiles, like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leatherback_turtle" title="Leatherback turtle" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA2U">leatherback turtles</a>, the low surface-to-volume ratio allows this metabolically produced heat to keep the animals warmer than their environment even though they do not have a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded" id="mwA2Y">warm-blooded</a> metabolism.<sup about="#mwt544" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-75" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-75"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-75" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 74;" id="mwA2c"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA2g">[74]</span></a></sup> This form of homeothermy is called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gigantothermy" title="Gigantothermy" id="mwA2k">gigantothermy</a>; it has been suggested as having been common in large <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwA2o">dinosaurs</a> and other extinct large-bodied reptiles.<sup about="#mwt548" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-76" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-76"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-76" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 75;" id="mwA2s"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA2w">[75]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt552" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-77" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-77"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-77" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 76;" id="mwA20"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA24">[76]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA28">The benefit of a low resting metabolism is that it requires far less fuel to sustain bodily functions. By using temperature variations in their surroundings, or by remaining cold when they do not need to move, reptiles can save considerable amounts of energy compared to endothermic animals of the same size.<sup about="#mwt558" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-78" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-78"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-78" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 77;" id="mwA3A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3E">[77]</span></a></sup> A crocodile needs from a tenth to a fifth of the food necessary for a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lion" title="Lion" id="mwA3I">lion</a> of the same weight and can live half a year without eating.<sup about="#mwt562" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Garnett_79-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Garnett"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Garnett-79"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Garnett-79" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 78;" id="mwA3M"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3Q">[78]</span></a></sup> Lower food requirements and adaptive metabolisms allow reptiles to dominate the animal life in regions where net <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Calorie" title="Calorie" id="mwA3U">calorie</a> availability is too low to sustain large-bodied mammals and birds.</p>

<p id="mwA3Y">It is generally assumed that reptiles are unable to produce the sustained high energy output necessary for long distance chases or flying.<sup about="#mwt570" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-80" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-80"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-80" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 79;" id="mwA3c"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3g">[79]</span></a></sup> Higher energetic capacity might have been responsible for the evolution of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Warm-blooded" title="Warm-blooded" id="mwA3k">warm-bloodedness</a> in birds and mammals.<sup about="#mwt574" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-81" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-81"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-81" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 80;" id="mwA3o"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA3s">[80]</span></a></sup> However, investigation of correlations between active capacity and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation" id="mwA3w">thermophysiology</a> show a weak relationship.<sup about="#mwt578" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-CGF1_82-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"CGF1"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-CGF1-82"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-CGF1-82" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 81;" id="mwA30"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA34">[81]</span></a></sup> Most extant reptiles are carnivores with a sit-and-wait feeding strategy; whether reptiles are cold blooded due to their ecology <!-- 'or because their metabolism is a result of their ecology' : As this it is just a restatement of 'reptiles are cold blooded due to their ecology', essentially it was saying whether A was because of B or A was because of B, which is stupid! --> is not clear. Energetic studies on some reptiles have shown active capacities equal to or greater than similar sized warm-blooded animals.<sup about="#mwt582" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-83" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-83"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-83" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 82;" id="mwA38"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA4A">[82]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="17" id="mwA4E"><h3 id="Respiratory_system">Respiratory system</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA4I"><span id="mwA4M"><video poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/220px--X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" height="147" width="220" resource="./File:X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_(Alligator_mississippiensis)_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv" id="mwA4Q"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv" type='video/ogg; codecs="vp8, vorbis"' data-width="360" data-height="240" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" id="mwA4o"/><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"' data-width="540" data-height="360" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" id="mwA4s"/><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.360p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"' data-width="540" data-height="360" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" id="mwA4w"/><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.480p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"' data-width="720" data-height="480" data-title="SD VP9 (480P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 480P" id="mwA40"/><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c3/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv/X-ray_video_of_a_female_American_alligator_%28Alligator_mississippiensis%29_while_breathing_-_pone.0004497.s009.ogv.480p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"' data-width="720" data-height="480" data-title="SD WebM (480P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 480P" id="mwA44"/></video></span><figcaption id="mwA48">X-ray <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Fluoroscopy" title="Fluoroscopy" id="mwA5A">fluoroscopy</a> videos of a female American alligator showing contraction of the lungs while breathing</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwA5E">All reptiles breathe using <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lung" title="Lung" id="mwA5I">lungs</a>. Aquatic <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle" title="Turtle" id="mwA5M">turtles</a> have developed more permeable skin, and some species have modified their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cloaca" title="Cloaca" id="mwA5Q">cloaca</a> to increase the area for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gas_exchange" title="Gas exchange" id="mwA5U">gas exchange</a>.<sup about="#mwt588" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-84" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-84"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-84" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 83;" id="mwA5Y"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA5c">[83]</span></a></sup> Even with these adaptations, breathing is never fully accomplished without lungs. Lung ventilation is accomplished differently in each main reptile group. In <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwA5g">squamates</a>, the lungs are ventilated almost exclusively by the axial musculature. This is also the same musculature that is used during locomotion. Because of this constraint, most squamates are forced to hold their breath during intense runs. Some, however, have found a way around it. Varanids, and a few other lizard species, employ <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Buccal_pumping" title="Buccal pumping" id="mwA5k">buccal pumping</a> as a complement to their normal "axial breathing". This allows the animals to completely fill their lungs during intense locomotion, and thus remain aerobically active for a long time. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tupinambis" title="Tupinambis" id="mwA5o">Tegu lizards</a> are known to possess a proto-<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm" id="mwA5s">diaphragm</a>, which separates the pulmonary cavity from the visceral cavity. While not actually capable of movement, it does allow for greater lung inflation, by taking the weight of the viscera off the lungs.<sup about="#mwt592" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-85" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-85"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-85" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 84;" id="mwA5w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA50">[84]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA54"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia" id="mwA58">Crocodilians</a> actually have a muscular diaphragm that is analogous to the mammalian diaphragm. The difference is that the muscles for the crocodilian diaphragm pull the pubis (part of the pelvis, which is movable in crocodilians) back, which brings the liver down, thus freeing space for the lungs to expand. This type of diaphragmatic setup has been referred to as the "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Liver" title="Liver" id="mwA6A">hepatic</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Piston" title="Piston" id="mwA6E">piston</a>". The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bronchus" title="Bronchus" id="mwA6I">airways</a> form a number of double tubular chambers within each lung. On inhalation and exhalation air moves through the airways in the same direction, thus creating a unidirectional airflow through the lungs. A similar system is found in birds,<sup about="#mwt599" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-86" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-86"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-86" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 85;" id="mwA6M"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA6Q">[85]</span></a></sup> monitor lizards<sup about="#mwt603" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-87" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-87"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-87" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 86;" id="mwA6U"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA6Y">[86]</span></a></sup> and iguanas.<sup about="#mwt608" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-88" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-88"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-88" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 87;" id="mwA6c"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA6g">[87]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA6k">Most reptiles lack a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Secondary_palate" title="Secondary palate" id="mwA6o">secondary palate</a>, meaning that they must hold their breath while swallowing. Crocodilians have evolved a bony secondary palate that allows them to continue breathing while remaining submerged (and protect their brains against damage by struggling prey). Skinks (family <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skink" title="Skink" id="mwA6s">Scincidae</a>) also have evolved a bony secondary palate, to varying degrees. Snakes took a different approach and extended their trachea instead. Their tracheal extension sticks out like a fleshy straw, and allows these animals to swallow large prey without suffering from asphyxiation.<sup about="#mwt613" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-89" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-89"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-89" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 88;" id="mwA6w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA60">[88]</span></a></sup></p>

<section data-mw-section-id="18" id="mwA64"><h4 id="Turtles_and_tortoises">Turtles and tortoises</h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA68"><a href="./File:Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA7A"><img resource="./File:Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg/220px-Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1701" data-file-height="1267" data-file-type="bitmap" height="164" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg/330px-Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg/440px-Tortue_de_Floride_Amiens.jpg 2x" id="mwA7E"/></a><figcaption id="mwA7I"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Red-eared_slider" title="Red-eared slider" id="mwA7M">Red-eared slider</a> taking a gulp of air</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwA7Q">How <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle" title="Turtle" id="mwA7U">turtles and tortoises</a> breathe has been the subject of much study. To date, only a few species have been studied thoroughly enough to get an idea of how those turtles <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Breathing" title="Breathing" id="mwA7Y">breathe</a>. The varied results indicate that turtles and tortoises have found a variety of solutions to this problem.</p>

<p id="mwA7c">The difficulty is that most <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle_shell" title="Turtle shell" id="mwA7g">turtle shells</a> are rigid and do not allow for the type of expansion and contraction that other amniotes use to ventilate their lungs. Some turtles, such as the Indian flapshell (<i id="mwA7k"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Indian_flapshell_turtle" title="Indian flapshell turtle" id="mwA7o">Lissemys punctata</a></i>), have a sheet of muscle that envelops the lungs. When it contracts, the turtle can exhale. When at rest, the turtle can retract the limbs into the body cavity and force air out of the lungs. When the turtle protracts its limbs, the pressure inside the lungs is reduced, and the turtle can suck air in. Turtle lungs are attached to the inside of the top of the shell (carapace), with the bottom of the lungs attached (via connective tissue) to the rest of the viscera. By using a series of special muscles (roughly equivalent to a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thoracic_diaphragm" title="Thoracic diaphragm" id="mwA7s">diaphragm</a>), turtles are capable of pushing their viscera up and down, resulting in effective respiration, since many of these muscles have attachment points in conjunction with their forelimbs (indeed, many of the muscles expand into the limb pockets during contraction).<sup about="#mwt619" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-90" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-90"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-90" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 89;" id="mwA7w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA70">[89]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwA74">Breathing during locomotion has been studied in three species, and they show different patterns. Adult female green sea turtles do not breathe as they crutch along their nesting beaches. They hold their breath during terrestrial locomotion and breathe in bouts as they rest. North American box turtles breathe continuously during locomotion, and the ventilation cycle is not coordinated with the limb movements.<sup about="#mwt625" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Landberg_91-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Landberg"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Landberg-91"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Landberg-91" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 90;" id="mwA78"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA8A">[90]</span></a></sup> This is because they use their abdominal muscles to breathe during locomotion. The last species to have been studied is the red-eared slider, which also breathes during locomotion, but takes smaller breaths during locomotion than during small pauses between locomotor bouts, indicating that there may be mechanical interference between the limb movements and the breathing apparatus. Box turtles have also been observed to breathe while completely sealed up inside their shells.<sup about="#mwt626" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Landberg_91-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Landberg"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Landberg-91" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 90;" id="mwA8E"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA8I">[90]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="19" id="mwA8M"><h3 id="Sound_production">Sound production</h3>
<p id="mwA8Q">Compared with frogs, birds, and mammals, reptiles are less vocal. Sound production is usually limited to <a rel="mw:WikiLink/Interwiki" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hiss" title="wikt:hiss" class="extiw" id="mwA8U">hissing</a>, which is produced merely by forcing air though a partly closed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Glottis" title="Glottis" id="mwA8Y">glottis</a> and is not considered to be a true vocalization. The ability to vocalize exists in crocodilians, some lizards and turtles; and typically involves vibrating fold-like structures in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Larynx" title="Larynx" id="mwA8c">larynx</a> or glottis. Some <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gecko" title="Gecko" id="mwA8g">geckos</a> and turtles possess true <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vocal_cord" title="Vocal cord" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA8k">vocal cords</a>, which have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Elastin" title="Elastin" id="mwA8o">elastin</a>-rich connective tissue.<sup about="#mwt632" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-92" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-92"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-92" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 91;" id="mwA8s"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA8w">[91]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt636" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-93" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-93"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-93" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 92;" id="mwA80"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA84">[92]</span></a></sup></p>

<section data-mw-section-id="20" id="mwA88"><h4 id="Hearing_in_snakes">Hearing in snakes</h4>
<p id="mwA9A">Hearing in humans relies on 3<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwA9E"> </span>parts of the ear; the outer ear that directs sound waves into the ear canal, the middle ear that transmits incoming sound waves to the inner ear, and the inner ear that helps in hearing and keeping your balance. Unlike humans and other mammals, snakes do not possess an outer ear, a middle ear, and a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tympanum_(anatomy)" title="Tympanum (anatomy)" id="mwA9I">tympanum</a> but have an inner ear structure with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cochlea" title="Cochlea" id="mwA9M">cochleas</a> directly connected to their jawbone.<sup about="#mwt642" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-94" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-94"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-94" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 93;" id="mwA9Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA9U">[93]</span></a></sup> They are able to feel the vibrations generated from the sound waves in their jaw as they move on the ground. This is done by the use of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mechanoreceptor" title="Mechanoreceptor" id="mwA9Y">mechanoreceptors</a>, sensory nerves that run along the body of snakes directing the vibrations along the spinal nerves to the brain. Snakes have a sensitive auditory perception and can tell which direction sound being made is coming from so that they can sense the presence of prey or predator but it is still unclear how sensitive snakes are to sound waves traveling through the air.<sup about="#mwt646" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-95" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-95"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-95" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 94;" id="mwA9c"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA9g">[94]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="21" id="mwA9k"><h3 id="Skin">Skin</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwA9o"><a href="./File:Lacertae_skin.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwA9s"><img resource="./File:Lacertae_skin.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lacertae_skin.jpg/220px-Lacertae_skin.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" data-file-type="bitmap" height="165" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lacertae_skin.jpg/330px-Lacertae_skin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lacertae_skin.jpg/440px-Lacertae_skin.jpg 2x" id="mwA9w"/></a><figcaption id="mwA90">Skin of a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sand_lizard" title="Sand lizard" id="mwA94">sand lizard</a>, showing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwA98">squamate reptiles</a> iconic <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scale_(zoology)" title="Scale (zoology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA-A">scales</a></figcaption></figure>
