<p id="mwA-E">Reptilian skin is covered in a horny <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)" id="mwA-I">epidermis</a>, making it watertight and enabling reptiles to live on dry land, in contrast to amphibians. Compared to mammalian skin, that of reptiles is rather thin and lacks the thick <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dermis" title="Dermis" id="mwA-M">dermal</a> layer that produces <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leather" title="Leather" id="mwA-Q">leather</a> in mammals.<sup about="#mwt651" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-96" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-96"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-96" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 95;" id="mwA-U"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA-Y">[95]</span></a></sup>
Exposed parts of reptiles are protected by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptile_scales" title="Reptile scales" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA-c">scales</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Scutes" title="Scutes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA-g">scutes</a>, sometimes with a bony base (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osteoderm" title="Osteoderm" id="mwA-k">osteoderms</a>), forming <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Armour_(zoology)" title="Armour (zoology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwA-o">armor</a>. In <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lepidosauria" title="Lepidosauria" id="mwA-s">lepidosaurians</a>, such as lizards and snakes, the whole skin is covered in overlapping <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Epidermis_(zoology)" title="Epidermis (zoology)" id="mwA-w">epidermal</a> scales. Such scales were once thought to be typical of the class Reptilia as a whole, but are now known to occur only in lepidosaurians.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt652" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"August 2015"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA-0"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_August_2015" about="#mwt652"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt652" id="mwA-4"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> The scales found in turtles and crocodiles are of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dermis" title="Dermis" id="mwA-8">dermal</a>, rather than epidermal, origin and are properly termed scutes.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt653" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"August 2015"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwA_A"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_August_2015" about="#mwt653"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt653" id="mwA_E"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> In turtles, the body is hidden inside a hard shell composed of fused scutes.</p>

<p id="mwA_I">Lacking a thick dermis, reptilian leather is not as strong as mammalian leather. It is used in leather-wares for decorative purposes for shoes, belts and handbags, particularly crocodile skin.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="22" id="mwA_M"><h4 id="Shedding">Shedding</h4>
<p id="mwA_Q">Reptiles shed their skin through a process called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ecdysis" title="Ecdysis" id="mwA_U">ecdysis</a> which occurs continuously throughout their lifetime. In particular, younger reptiles tend to shed once every 5–6 weeks while adults shed 3–4 times a year.<sup about="#mwt663" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-97" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-97"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-97" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 96;" id="mwA_Y"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA_c">[96]</span></a></sup> Younger reptiles shed more because of their rapid growth rate. Once full size, the frequency of shedding drastically decreases. The process of ecdysis involves forming a new layer of skin under the old one. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis" id="mwA_g">Proteolytic</a> enzymes and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lymph" title="Lymph" id="mwA_k">lymphatic fluid</a> is secreted between the old and new layers of skin. Consequently, this lifts the old skin from the new one allowing shedding to occur.<sup about="#mwt667" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards_98-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Dermatological Diseases in Lizards"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards-98"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards-98" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 97;" id="mwA_o"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA_s">[97]</span></a></sup> Snakes will shed from the head to the tail while lizards shed in a "patchy pattern".<sup about="#mwt668" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards_98-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Dermatological Diseases in Lizards"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards-98" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 97;" id="mwA_w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA_0">[97]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dysecdysis?action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dysecdysis" class="new" typeof="mw:LocalizedAttrs" data-mw-i18n='{"title":{"lang":"x-page","key":"red-link-title","params":["Dysecdysis"]}}' id="mwA_4">Dysecdysis</a>, a common skin disease in snakes and lizards, will occur when ecdysis, or shedding, fails.<sup about="#mwt672" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Veterinary_Nursing_of_Exotic_Pets_99-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Veterinary Nursing of Exotic Pets"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Veterinary_Nursing_of_Exotic_Pets-99"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Veterinary_Nursing_of_Exotic_Pets-99" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 98;" id="mwA_8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBAA">[98]</span></a></sup> There are numerous reasons why shedding fails and can be related to inadequate humidity and temperature, nutritional deficiencies, dehydration and traumatic injuries.<sup about="#mwt673" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards_98-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Dermatological Diseases in Lizards"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Dermatological_Diseases_in_Lizards-98" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 97;" id="mwBAE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBAI">[97]</span></a></sup> Nutritional deficiencies decrease proteolytic enzymes while dehydration reduces lymphatic fluids to separate the skin layers. Traumatic injuries on the other hand, form scars that will not allow new scales to form and disrupt the process of ecdysis.<sup about="#mwt674" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Veterinary_Nursing_of_Exotic_Pets_99-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Veterinary Nursing of Exotic Pets"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Veterinary_Nursing_of_Exotic_Pets-99" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 98;" id="mwBAM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBAQ">[98]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="23" id="mwBAU"><h3 id="Excretion">Excretion</h3>
<p id="mwBAY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Excretion" title="Excretion" id="mwBAc">Excretion</a> is performed mainly by two small <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kidney" title="Kidney" id="mwBAg">kidneys</a>. In diapsids, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Uric_acid" title="Uric acid" id="mwBAk">uric acid</a> is the main <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen" id="mwBAo">nitrogenous</a> waste product; turtles, like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammal" title="Mammal" id="mwBAs">mammals</a>, excrete mainly <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Urea" title="Urea" id="mwBAw">urea</a>. Unlike the kidneys of mammals and birds, reptile kidneys are unable to produce liquid urine more concentrated than their body fluid. This is because they lack a specialized structure called a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Loop_of_Henle" title="Loop of Henle" id="mwBA0">loop of Henle</a>, which is present in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nephron" title="Nephron" id="mwBA4">nephrons</a> of birds and mammals. Because of this, many reptiles use the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Colon_(anatomy)" title="Colon (anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBA8">colon</a> to aid in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reabsorption" title="Reabsorption" id="mwBBA">reabsorption</a> of water. Some are also able to take up water stored in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Urinary_bladder" title="Urinary bladder" id="mwBBE">bladder</a>. Excess salts are also excreted by nasal and lingual <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salt_gland" title="Salt gland" id="mwBBI">salt glands</a> in some reptiles.</p>

<p id="mwBBM">In all reptiles the urinogenital ducts and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anus" title="Anus" id="mwBBQ">anus</a> both empty into an organ called a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cloaca" title="Cloaca" id="mwBBU">cloaca</a>. In some reptiles, a midventral wall in the cloaca may open into a urinary bladder, but not all. It is present in all turtles and tortoises as well as most lizards, but is lacking in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard" id="mwBBY">monitor lizard</a>, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Legless_lizard" title="Legless lizard" id="mwBBc">legless lizards</a>. It is absent in the snakes, alligators, and crocodiles.<sup about="#mwt679" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-100" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-100"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-100" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 99;" id="mwBBg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBBk">[99]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBBo">Many turtles, tortoises, and lizards have proportionally very large bladders. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin" id="mwBBs">Charles Darwin</a> noted that the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Galapagos_tortoise" title="Galapagos tortoise" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBBw">Galapagos tortoise</a> had a bladder which could store up to 20% of its body weight.<sup about="#mwt687" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-101" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-101"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-101" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 100;" id="mwBB0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBB4">[100]</span></a></sup> Such adaptations are the result of environments such as remote islands and deserts where water is very scarce.<sup about="#mwt691" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-102" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-102"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-102" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 101;" id="mwBB8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCA">[101]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap" about="#mwt683" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"rp","href":"./Template:Rp"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"143"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBCE"><span title="Page / location: 143"><span typeof="mw:Entity">:</span><span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>143<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span></span></sup> Other desert-dwelling reptiles have large bladders that can store a long-term reservoir of water for up to several months and aid in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation" id="mwBCI">osmoregulation</a>.<sup about="#mwt695" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-103" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-103"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-103" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 102;" id="mwBCM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCQ">[102]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBCU">Turtles have two or more accessory urinary bladders, located lateral to the neck of the urinary bladder and dorsal to the pubis, occupying a significant portion of their body cavity.<sup about="#mwt701" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-104" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-104"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-104" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 103;" id="mwBCY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCc">[103]</span></a></sup> Their bladder is also usually bilobed with a left and right section. The right section is located under the liver, which prevents large stones from remaining in that side while the left section is more likely to have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bladder_stone_(animal)" title="Bladder stone (animal)" id="mwBCg">calculi</a>.<sup about="#mwt705" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-105" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-105"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-105" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 104;" id="mwBCk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBCo">[104]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="24" id="mwBCs"><h3 id="Digestion">Digestion</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBCw"><a href="./File:PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBC0"><img resource="./File:PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg/220px-PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="2448" data-file-type="bitmap" height="220" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg/330px-PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg/440px-PikiWiki_Israel_37648_Nature_and_Colors.jpg 2x" id="mwBC4"/></a><figcaption id="mwBC8">A colubrid snake, <i id="mwBDA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dolichophis_jugularis" title="Dolichophis jugularis" id="mwBDE">Dolichophis jugularis</a></i>, eating a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sheltopusik" title="Sheltopusik" id="mwBDI">legless lizard</a>, <i id="mwBDM">Pseudopus apodus</i>. Most reptiles are carnivorous, and many primarily eat other reptiles and small mammals.</figcaption></figure>
<!--[[File:Watersnake.JPG|thumb|Watersnake ''[[Malpolon monspessulanus]]'' eating a lizard.]]-->
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBDQ"><a href="./File:PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBDU"><img resource="./File:PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg/220px-PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1805" data-file-height="1381" data-file-type="bitmap" height="168" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg/330px-PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg/440px-PSM_V53_D226_Silicious_pebbles_from_the_stomach_of_a_plesiosaur.jpg 2x" id="mwBDY"/></a><figcaption id="mwBDc"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith" id="mwBDg">Gastroliths</a> from a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plesiosaur" title="Plesiosaur" id="mwBDk">plesiosaur</a></figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwBDo">Most reptiles are insectivorous or carnivorous and have simple and comparatively short digestive tracts due to meat being fairly simple to break down and digest. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Digestion" title="Digestion" id="mwBDs">Digestion</a> is slower than in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mammals" title="Mammals" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBDw">mammals</a>, reflecting their lower resting <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Metabolism" title="Metabolism" id="mwBD0">metabolism</a> and their inability to divide and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Masticate" title="Masticate" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBD4">masticate</a> their food.<sup about="#mwt711" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-106" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-106"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-106" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 105;" id="mwBD8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEA">[105]</span></a></sup> Their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Poikilotherm" title="Poikilotherm" id="mwBEE">poikilotherm</a> metabolism has very low energy requirements, allowing large reptiles like crocodiles and large constrictors to live from a single large meal for months, digesting it slowly.<sup about="#mwt712" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Garnett_79-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Garnett"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Garnett-79" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 78;" id="mwBEI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEM">[78]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBEQ">While modern reptiles are predominantly carnivorous, during the early history of reptiles several groups produced some herbivorous <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Megafauna" title="Megafauna" id="mwBEU">megafauna</a>: in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic" id="mwBEY">Paleozoic</a>, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pareiasaur" title="Pareiasaur" id="mwBEc">pareiasaurs</a>; and in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic" id="mwBEg">Mesozoic</a> several lines of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwBEk">dinosaurs</a>.<sup about="#mwt715" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010_44-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Sahney-Benton-Ferry-2010-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mwBEo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBEs">[43]</span></a></sup> Today, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Turtle" title="Turtle" id="mwBEw">turtles</a> are the only predominantly herbivorous reptile group, but several lines of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Agamidae" title="Agamidae" id="mwBE0">agamas</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Iguanidae" title="Iguanidae" id="mwBE4">iguanas</a> have evolved to live wholly or partly on plants.<sup about="#mwt719" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-herbivory_107-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"herbivory"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-herbivory-107"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-herbivory-107" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 106;" id="mwBE8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFA">[106]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBFE">Herbivorous reptiles face the same problems of mastication as herbivorous mammals but, lacking the complex teeth of mammals, many species swallow rocks and pebbles (so called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gastrolith" title="Gastrolith" id="mwBFI">gastroliths</a>) to aid in digestion: The rocks are washed around in the stomach, helping to grind up plant matter.<sup about="#mwt725" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-herbivory_107-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"herbivory"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-herbivory-107" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 106;" id="mwBFM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFQ">[106]</span></a></sup> Fossil gastroliths have been found associated with both <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBFU">ornithopods</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauropods" title="Sauropods" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBFY">sauropods</a>, though whether they actually functioned as a gastric mill in the latter is disputed.<sup about="#mwt729" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-108" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-108"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-108" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 107;" id="mwBFc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFg">[107]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt734" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-109" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-109"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-109" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 108;" id="mwBFk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBFo">[108]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Salt_water_crocodile" title="Salt water crocodile" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBFs">Salt water crocodiles</a> also use gastroliths as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sailing_ballast" title="Sailing ballast" id="mwBFw">ballast</a>, stabilizing them in the water or helping them to dive.<sup about="#mwt738" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-110" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-110"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-110" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 109;" id="mwBF0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBF4">[109]</span></a></sup> A dual function as both stabilizing ballast and digestion aid has been suggested for gastroliths found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plesiosaur" title="Plesiosaur" id="mwBF8">plesiosaurs</a>.<sup about="#mwt743" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-111" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-111"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-111" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 110;" id="mwBGA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGE">[110]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="25" id="mwBGI"><h3 id="Nerves">Nerves</h3>
<p id="mwBGM">The reptilian nervous system contains the same basic part of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amphibian" title="Amphibian" id="mwBGQ">amphibian</a> brain, but the reptile <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cerebrum" title="Cerebrum" id="mwBGU">cerebrum</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum" id="mwBGY">cerebellum</a> are slightly larger. Most typical sense organs are well developed with certain exceptions, most notably the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snake" title="Snake" id="mwBGc">snake</a>'s lack of external ears (middle and inner ears are present). There are twelve pairs of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cranial_nerves" title="Cranial nerves" id="mwBGg">cranial nerves</a>.<sup about="#mwt748" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-112" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-112"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-112" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 111;" id="mwBGk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBGo">[111]</span></a></sup> Due to their short cochlea, reptiles use <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Electrical_tuning" title="Electrical tuning" id="mwBGs">electrical tuning</a> to expand their range of audible frequencies.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="26" id="mwBGw"><h4 id="Intelligence">Intelligence</h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt749" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"see also","href":"./Template:See_also"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Animal cognition"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBG0"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt749" id="mwBG4">See also: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Animal_cognition" title="Animal cognition">Animal cognition</a></div>

<p id="mwBG8">Reptiles are generally considered less intelligent than mammals and birds.<sup about="#mwt762" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Romer,_A_1977_30-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Romer, A 1977"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Romer,_A_1977-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwBHA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBHE">[29]</span></a></sup> The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Brain-to-body_mass_ratio" title="Brain-to-body mass ratio" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBHI">size of their brain relative to their body</a> is much less than that of mammals, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Encephalization_quotient" title="Encephalization quotient" id="mwBHM">encephalization quotient</a> being about one tenth of that of mammals,<sup about="#mwt766" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-113" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-113"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-113" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 112;" id="mwBHQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBHU">[112]</span></a></sup> though larger reptiles can show more complex brain development. Larger lizards, like the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard" id="mwBHY">monitors</a>, are known to exhibit complex behavior, including cooperation<sup about="#mwt770" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-114" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-114"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-114" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 113;" id="mwBHc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBHg">[113]</span></a></sup> and cognitive abilities allowing them to optimize their <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Foraging" title="Foraging" id="mwBHk">foraging</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)" id="mwBHo">territoriality</a> over time.<sup about="#mwt774" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-115" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-115"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-115" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 114;" id="mwBHs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBHw">[114]</span></a></sup> Crocodiles have relatively larger brains and show a fairly complex social structure. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Komodo_dragon" title="Komodo dragon" id="mwBH0">Komodo dragon</a> is even known to engage in play,<sup about="#mwt778" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-firefly_116-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"firefly"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-firefly-116"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-firefly-116" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 115;" id="mwBH4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBH8">[115]</span></a></sup> as are turtles, which are also considered to be social creatures,<sup about="#mwt782" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-117" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-117"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-117" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 116;" id="mwBIA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBIE">[116]</span></a></sup> and sometimes switch between monogamy and promiscuity in their sexual behavior.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt761" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"May 2017"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBII"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_May_2017" about="#mwt761"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt761" id="mwBIM"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> One study found that <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wood_turtle" title="Wood turtle" id="mwBIQ">wood turtles</a> were better than <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./White_rat" title="White rat" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBIU">white rats</a> at learning to navigate mazes.<sup about="#mwt786" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-118" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-118"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-118" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 117;" id="mwBIY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBIc">[117]</span></a></sup> Another study found that giant tortoises are capable of learning through <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Operant_conditioning" title="Operant conditioning" id="mwBIg">operant conditioning</a>, visual discrimination and retained learned behaviors with long-term memory.<sup about="#mwt790" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-119" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-119"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-119" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 118;" id="mwBIk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBIo">[118]</span></a></sup> Sea turtles have been regarded as having simple brains, but their flippers are used for a variety of foraging tasks (holding, bracing, corralling) in common with marine mammals.<sup about="#mwt794" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-120" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-120"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-120" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 119;" id="mwBIs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBIw">[119]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="27" id="mwBI0"><h3 id="Vision">Vision</h3>
<p id="mwBI4">Most reptiles are <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Diurnality" title="Diurnality" id="mwBI8">diurnal</a> animals. The vision is typically adapted to daylight conditions, with color vision and more advanced visual <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Depth_perception" title="Depth perception" id="mwBJA">depth perception</a> than in amphibians and most mammals.</p>

<p id="mwBJE">Reptiles usually have excellent vision, allowing them to detect shapes and motions at long distances. They often have only a few <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rod_cell" title="Rod cell" id="mwBJI">Rod cells</a> and have poor vision in low-light conditions. At the same time they have cells called "double <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cone_cell" title="Cone cell" id="mwBJM">cones</a>" which give them sharp color vision and enable them to see <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet" id="mwBJQ">ultraviolet</a> wavelengths.<sup about="#mwt799" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Brames_121-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Brames"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Brames-121"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Brames-121" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 120;" id="mwBJU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBJY">[120]</span></a></sup> In some species, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Blind_snake" title="Blind snake" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBJc">blind snakes</a>, vision is reduced.</p>

<p id="mwBJg">Many <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lepidosauria" title="Lepidosauria" id="mwBJk">lepidosaurs</a> have a photosensory organ on the top of their heads called the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parietal_eye" title="Parietal eye" id="mwBJo">parietal eye</a>, which are also called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Third_eye" title="Third eye" id="mwBJs">third eye</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pineal_eye" title="Pineal eye" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBJw">pineal eye</a> or <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pineal_gland" title="Pineal gland" id="mwBJ0">pineal gland</a>. This "eye" does not work the same way as a normal eye does as it has only a rudimentary retina and lens and thus, cannot form images. It is, however, sensitive to changes in light and dark and can detect movement.<sup about="#mwt801" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Brames_121-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Brames"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Brames-121" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 120;" id="mwBJ4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBJ8">[120]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBKA">Some snakes have extra sets of visual organs (in the loosest sense of the word) in the form of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Loreal_pit" title="Loreal pit" id="mwBKE">pits</a> sensitive to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Infrared" title="Infrared" id="mwBKI">infrared</a> radiation (heat). Such heat-sensitive pits are particularly well developed in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pit_vipers" title="Pit vipers" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBKM">pit vipers</a>, but are also found in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Boidae" title="Boidae" id="mwBKQ">boas</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pythonidae" title="Pythonidae" id="mwBKU">pythons</a>. These pits allow the snakes to sense the body heat of birds and mammals, enabling pit vipers to hunt rodents in the dark.<sup about="#mwt803" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-123" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"efn","href":"./Template:Efn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"\n\"The copperhead is a pit viper and, like others pit vipers, it has heat-sensitive pit organs on each side of its head between the eye and the nostril. These pits detect objects that are warmer than the environment and enable copperheads to locate nocturnal, mammalian prey.\"&lt;ref>{{cite web |title=Northern copperhead |date=25 April 2016 |website=Smithsonian&apos;s National Zoo &amp; Conservation Biology Institute |url=https://nationalzoo.si.edu/animals/northern-copperhead |access-date=12 February 2020}}&lt;/ref>\n"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-123" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 2;" data-mw-group="lower-alpha" id="mwBKY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBKc">[lower-alpha 2]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBKg">Most reptiles, as well as birds, possess a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane" id="mwBKk">nictitating membrane</a>, a translucent third eyelid which is drawn over the eye from the inner corner. In crocodilians , it protects its eyeball surface while allowing a degree of vision underwater.<sup about="#mwt816" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-124" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-124"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-124" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 122;" id="mwBKo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBKs">[122]</span></a></sup> However, many squamates, geckos and snakes in particular, lack eyelids, which are replaced by a transparent scale. This is called the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Brille" title="Brille" id="mwBKw">brille</a>, spectacle, or eyecap. The brille is usually not visible, except for when the snake molts, and it protects the eyes from dust and dirt.<sup about="#mwt820" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-125" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-125"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-125" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 123;" id="mwBK0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBK4">[123]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="28" id="mwBK8"><h3 id="Reproduction">Reproduction</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBLA"><a href="./File:Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBLE"><img resource="./File:Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg/220px-Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg.png" decoding="async" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" data-file-type="drawing" height="165" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg/330px-Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg/440px-Crocodile_Egg_Diagram.svg.png 2x" id="mwBLI"/></a><figcaption id="mwBLM">Crocodilian egg diagram<br id="mwBLQ"/>
(1)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLU"> </span>eggshell, (2)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLY"> </span>yolk sac, (3)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLc"> </span>yolk (nutrients), (4)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLg"> </span>vessels, (5)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLk"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amnion" title="Amnion" id="mwBLo">amnion</a>, (6)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBLs"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chorion" title="Chorion" id="mwBLw">chorion</a>, (7)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBL0"> </span>air space, (8)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBL4"> </span><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Allantois" title="Allantois" id="mwBL8">allantois</a>, (9)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBMA"> </span>albumin (egg white), (10)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBME"> </span>amniotic sac, (11)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBMI"> </span>crocodile embryo, (12)<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBMM"> </span>amniotic fluid</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBMQ"><a href="./File:Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating,_ventral_view.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBMU"><img resource="./File:Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating,_ventral_view.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg/220px-Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="3353" data-file-height="5030" data-file-type="bitmap" height="330" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg/330px-Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg/440px-Hemidactylus_frenatus_mating%2C_ventral_view.jpg 2x" id="mwBMY"/></a><figcaption id="mwBMc"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Common_house_gecko" title="Common house gecko" id="mwBMg">Common house geckos</a> mating, ventral view with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hemipenis" title="Hemipenis" id="mwBMk">hemipenis</a> inserted in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cloaca" title="Cloaca" id="mwBMo">cloaca</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBMs"><a href="./File:Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBMw"><img resource="./File:Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg/220px-Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1731" data-file-height="912" data-file-type="bitmap" height="116" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg/330px-Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg/440px-Trachylepis_maculilabris_mating.jpg 2x" id="mwBM0"/></a><figcaption id="mwBM4">Most reptiles reproduce sexually, for example this <i id="mwBM8">Trachylepis maculilabris</i> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skink" title="Skink" id="mwBNA">skink</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBNE"><a href="./File:Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBNI"><img resource="./File:Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg/220px-Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1204" data-file-height="1204" data-file-type="bitmap" height="220" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg/330px-Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg/440px-Tortoise-Hatchling.jpg 2x" id="mwBNM"/></a><figcaption id="mwBNQ">Reptiles have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amniote" title="Amniote" id="mwBNU">amniotic</a> eggs with hard or leathery shells, requiring <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Internal_fertilization" title="Internal fertilization" id="mwBNY">internal fertilization</a> when mating.</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwBNc">Reptiles generally <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sexual_reproduction" title="Sexual reproduction" id="mwBNg">reproduce sexually</a>,<sup about="#mwt826" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-126" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-126"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-126" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 124;" id="mwBNk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBNo">[124]</span></a></sup> though some are capable of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction" id="mwBNs">asexual reproduction</a>. All reproductive activity occurs through the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cloaca" title="Cloaca" id="mwBNw">cloaca</a>, the single exit/entrance at the base of the tail where waste is also eliminated. Most reptiles have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Copulatory_organ" title="Copulatory organ" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBN0">copulatory organs</a>, which are usually retracted or inverted and stored inside the body. In turtles and crocodilians, the male has a single median <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Penis" title="Penis" id="mwBN4">penis</a>, while squamates, including snakes and lizards, possess a pair of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hemipenis" title="Hemipenis" id="mwBN8">hemipenes</a>, only one of which is typically used in each session. Tuatara, however, lack copulatory organs, and so the male and female simply press their cloacas together as the male discharges sperm.<sup about="#mwt830" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-127" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-127"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-127" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 125;" id="mwBOA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBOE">[125]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBOI">Most reptiles lay amniotic eggs covered with leathery or calcareous shells. An <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Amnion" title="Amnion" id="mwBOM">amnion</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOQ"> </span>(5), <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chorion" title="Chorion" id="mwBOU">chorion</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOY"> </span>(6), and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Allantois" title="Allantois" id="mwBOc">allantois</a><span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOg"> </span>(8) are present during <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Embryo" title="Embryo" id="mwBOk">embryonic</a> life. The eggshell<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOo"> </span>(1) protects the crocodile embryo<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOs"> </span>(11) and keeps it from drying out, but it is flexible to allow gas exchange. The chorion<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBOw"> </span>(6) aids in gas exchange between the inside and outside of the egg. It allows carbon dioxide to exit the egg and oxygen gas to enter the egg. The albumin<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBO0"> </span>(9) further protects the embryo and serves as a reservoir for water and protein. The allantois<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBO4"> </span>(8) is a sac that collects the metabolic waste produced by the embryo. The amniotic sac<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBO8"> </span>(10) contains amniotic fluid<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPA"> </span>(12) which protects and cushions the embryo. The amnion<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPE"> </span>(5) aids in osmoregulation and serves as a saltwater reservoir. The yolk sac<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPI"> </span>(2) surrounding the yolk<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPM"> </span>(3) contains protein and fat rich nutrients that are absorbed by the embryo via vessels<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPQ"> </span>(4) that allow the embryo to grow and metabolize. The air space<span typeof="mw:Entity" id="mwBPU"> </span>(7) provides the embryo with oxygen while it is hatching. This ensures that the embryo will not suffocate while it is hatching. There are no <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Larva" title="Larva" id="mwBPY">larval</a> stages of development. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Viviparity" title="Viviparity" id="mwBPc">Viviparity</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ovoviviparity" title="Ovoviviparity" id="mwBPg">ovoviviparity</a> have evolved in many extinct clades of reptiles and in squamates. In the latter group, many species, including all boas and most vipers, use this mode of reproduction. The degree of viviparity varies; some species simply retain the eggs until just before hatching, others provide maternal nourishment to supplement the yolk, and yet others lack any yolk and provide all nutrients via a structure similar to the mammalian <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Placenta" title="Placenta" id="mwBPk">placenta</a>. The earliest documented case of viviparity in reptiles is the Early <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Permian" title="Permian" id="mwBPo">Permian</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesosaur" title="Mesosaur" id="mwBPs">mesosaurs</a>,<sup about="#mwt836" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-PFML12_128-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"PFML12"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-PFML12-128"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-PFML12-128" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 126;" id="mwBPw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBP0">[126]</span></a></sup> although some individuals or taxa in that clade may also have been oviparous because a putative isolated egg has also been found. Several groups of Mesozoic marine reptiles also exhibited viviparity, such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mosasaur" title="Mosasaur" id="mwBP4">mosasaurs</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ichthyosaur" title="Ichthyosaur" id="mwBP8">ichthyosaurs</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sauropterygia" title="Sauropterygia" id="mwBQA">Sauropterygia</a>, a group that include <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pachypleurosaur" title="Pachypleurosaur" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBQE">pachypleurosaurs</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plesiosauria" title="Plesiosauria" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBQI">Plesiosauria</a>.<sup about="#mwt837" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-S12_4-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"S12"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-S12-4" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 4;" id="mwBQM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBQQ">[4]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBQU">Asexual reproduction has been identified in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Squamata" title="Squamata" id="mwBQY">squamates</a> in six families of lizards and one snake. In some species of squamates, a population of females is able to produce a unisexual diploid clone of the mother. This form of asexual reproduction, called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis" id="mwBQc">parthenogenesis</a>, occurs in several species of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gecko" title="Gecko" id="mwBQg">gecko</a>, and is particularly widespread in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teiidae" title="Teiidae" id="mwBQk">teiids</a> (especially <i id="mwBQo">Aspidocelis</i>) and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lacertidae" title="Lacertidae" id="mwBQs">lacertids</a> (<i id="mwBQw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lacerta_(genus)" title="Lacerta (genus)" id="mwBQ0">Lacerta</a></i>). In captivity, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Komodo_dragon" title="Komodo dragon" id="mwBQ4">Komodo dragons</a> (Varanidae) have reproduced by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Parthenogenesis" title="Parthenogenesis" id="mwBQ8">parthenogenesis</a>.</p>

<p id="mwBRA">Parthenogenetic species are suspected to occur among <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chameleon" title="Chameleon" id="mwBRE">chameleons</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Agamidae" title="Agamidae" id="mwBRI">agamids</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Night_lizard" title="Night lizard" id="mwBRM">xantusiids</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Typhlopidae" title="Typhlopidae" id="mwBRQ">typhlopids</a>.</p>

<p id="mwBRU">Some reptiles exhibit <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Temperature-dependent_sex_determination" title="Temperature-dependent sex determination" id="mwBRY">temperature-dependent sex determination</a> (TDSD), in which the incubation temperature determines whether a particular egg hatches as male or female. TDSD is most common in turtles and crocodiles, but also occurs in lizards and tuatara.<sup about="#mwt843" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-129" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-129"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-129" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 127;" id="mwBRc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBRg">[127]</span></a></sup> To date, there has been no confirmation of whether TDSD occurs in snakes.<sup about="#mwt847" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-130" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-130"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-130" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 128;" id="mwBRk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBRo">[128]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="29" id="mwBRs"><h2 id="Defense_mechanisms">Defense mechanisms</h2>
<p id="mwBRw">Many small reptiles, such as snakes and lizards, that live on the ground or in the water are vulnerable to being preyed on by all kinds of carnivorous animals. Thus, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Conflict_avoidance" title="Conflict avoidance" id="mwBR0">avoidance</a> is the most common form of defense in reptiles.<sup about="#mwt852" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-131" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-131"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-131" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 129;" id="mwBR4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBR8">[129]</span></a></sup> At the first sign of danger, most snakes and lizards crawl away into the undergrowth, and turtles and crocodiles will plunge into water and sink out of sight.</p>

<section data-mw-section-id="30" id="mwBSA"><h3 id="Camouflage_and_warning">Camouflage and warning</h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBSE"><a href="./File:Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBSI"><img resource="./File:Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg/220px-Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" data-file-type="bitmap" height="147" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg/330px-Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg/440px-Phelsuma_dubia_edit1.jpg 2x" id="mwBSM"/></a><figcaption id="mwBSQ">A camouflaged <i id="mwBSU"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dull_day_gecko" title="Dull day gecko" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBSY">Phelsuma deubia</a></i> on a palm frond</figcaption></figure>

<p id="mwBSc">Reptiles tend to avoid confrontation through <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Camouflage" title="Camouflage" id="mwBSg">camouflage</a>. Two major groups of reptile predators are birds and other reptiles, both of which have well developed color vision. Thus the skins of many reptiles have <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crypsis" title="Crypsis" id="mwBSk">cryptic</a> coloration of plain or mottled gray, green, and brown to allow them to blend into the background of their natural environment.<sup about="#mwt858" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-132" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-132"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-132" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 130;" id="mwBSo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBSs">[130]</span></a></sup> Aided by the reptiles' capacity for remaining motionless for long periods, the camouflage of many snakes is so effective that people or domestic animals are most typically bitten because they accidentally step on them.<sup about="#mwt862" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-133" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-133"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-133" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 131;" id="mwBSw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBS0">[131]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBS4">When camouflage fails to protect them, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Blue-tongued_skink" title="Blue-tongued skink" id="mwBS8">blue-tongued skinks</a> will try to ward off attackers by displaying their blue tongues, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Frill-necked_lizard" title="Frill-necked lizard" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBTA">frill-necked lizard</a> will display its brightly colored frill. These same displays are used in territorial disputes and during courtship.<sup about="#mwt867" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Cogger_134-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Cogger"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Cogger-134"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Cogger-134" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 132;" id="mwBTE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBTI">[132]</span></a></sup> If danger arises so suddenly that flight is useless, crocodiles, turtles, some lizards, and some snakes hiss loudly when confronted by an enemy. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Rattlesnakes" title="Rattlesnakes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBTM">Rattlesnakes</a> rapidly vibrate the tip of the tail, which is composed of a series of nested, hollow beads to ward off approaching danger.</p>

<p id="mwBTQ">In contrast to the normal drab coloration of most reptiles, the lizards of the genus <i id="mwBTU">Heloderma</i> (the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gila_monster" title="Gila monster" id="mwBTY">Gila monster</a> and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Beaded_lizard" title="Beaded lizard" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBTc">beaded lizard</a>) and many of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coral_snake" title="Coral snake" id="mwBTg">coral snakes</a> have high-contrast warning coloration, warning potential predators they are venomous.<sup about="#mwt874" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-135" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-135"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-135" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 133;" id="mwBTk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBTo">[133]</span></a></sup> A number of non-venomous North American snake species have colorful markings similar to those of the coral snake, an oft cited example of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Batesian_mimicry" title="Batesian mimicry" id="mwBTs">Batesian mimicry</a>.<sup about="#mwt879" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-136" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-136"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-136" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 134;" id="mwBTw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBT0">[134]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt884" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-137" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-137"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-137" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 135;" id="mwBT4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBT8">[135]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="31" id="mwBUA"><h3 id="Alternative_defense_in_snakes">Alternative defense in snakes</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt885" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Venom"},"2":{"wt":"Evolution of snake venom"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBUE"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt885" id="mwBUI">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Venom" title="Venom">Venom</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Evolution_of_snake_venom" title="Evolution of snake venom">Evolution of snake venom</a></div>

<p id="mwBUM">Camouflage does not always fool a predator. When caught out, snake species adopt different defensive tactics and use a complicated set of behaviors when attacked. Some species, like cobras or hognose snakes, first elevate their head and spread out the skin of their neck in an effort to look large and threatening. Failure of this strategy may lead to other measures practiced particularly by cobras, vipers, and closely related species, which use <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snake_venom" title="Snake venom" id="mwBUQ">venom</a> to attack. The venom is modified saliva, delivered through fangs from a venom gland.<sup about="#mwt895" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-138" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-138"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-138" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 136;" id="mwBUU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBUY">[136]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt899" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-139" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-139"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-139" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 137;" id="mwBUc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBUg">[137]</span></a></sup> Some non-venomous snakes, such as American <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hognose_snakes" title="Hognose snakes" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBUk">hognose snakes</a> or European <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Grass_snake" title="Grass snake" id="mwBUo">grass snake</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thanatosis" title="Thanatosis" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBUs">play dead</a> when in danger; some, including the grass snake, exude a foul-smelling liquid to deter attackers.<sup about="#mwt903" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-140" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-140"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-140" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 138;" id="mwBUw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBU0">[138]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt907" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-141" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-141"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-141" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 139;" id="mwBU4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBU8">[139]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="32" id="mwBVA"><h3 id="Defense_in_crocodilians"><span class="anchor" id="Defense_in_crocodiles" about="#mwt908" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"anchor","href":"./Template:Anchor"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Defense in crocodiles"}},"i":0}}]}'></span>Defense in crocodilians</h3>
<p id="mwBVE">When a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crocodilian" title="Crocodilian" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBVI">crocodilian</a> is concerned about its safety, it will gape to expose the teeth and tongue. If this does not work, the crocodilian gets a little more agitated and typically begins to make hissing sounds. After this, the crocodilian will start to change its posture dramatically to make itself look more intimidating. The body is inflated to increase apparent size. If absolutely necessary it may decide to attack an enemy.</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBVM"><a href="./File:White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBVQ"><img resource="./File:White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg/220px-White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1200" data-file-type="bitmap" height="147" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg/330px-White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg/440px-White-headed_dwarf_gecko.jpg 2x" id="mwBVU"/></a><figcaption id="mwBVY">A <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./White-headed_dwarf_gecko" title="White-headed dwarf gecko" id="mwBVc">White-headed dwarf gecko</a> with shed tail</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwBVg">Some species try to bite immediately. Some will use their heads as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sledgehammer" title="Sledgehammer" id="mwBVk">sledgehammers</a> and literally smash an opponent, some will rush or swim toward the threat from a distance, even chasing the opponent onto land or galloping after it.<sup about="#mwt914" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-142" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-142"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-142" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 140;" id="mwBVo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBVs">[140]</span></a></sup> The main weapon in all crocodiles is the bite, which can generate very high bite force. Many species also possess <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth" id="mwBVw">canine</a>-like teeth. These are used primarily for seizing prey, but are also used in fighting and display.<sup about="#mwt919" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-143" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-143"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-143" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 141;" id="mwBV0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBV4">[141]</span></a></sup></p>

</section><section data-mw-section-id="33" id="mwBV8"><h3 id="Shedding_and_regenerating_tails">Shedding and regenerating tails</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt920" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"Main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Autotomy"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBWA"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt920" id="mwBWE">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Autotomy" title="Autotomy">Autotomy</a></div>
<p id="mwBWI"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Geckos" title="Geckos" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBWM">Geckos</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skinks" title="Skinks" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBWQ">skinks</a>, and some other lizards that are captured by the tail will shed part of the tail structure through a process called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Autotomy" title="Autotomy" id="mwBWU">autotomy</a> and thus be able to flee. The detached tail will continue to thrash, creating a deceptive sense of continued struggle and distracting the predator's attention from the fleeing prey animal. The detached tails of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Leopard_gecko" title="Leopard gecko" id="mwBWY">leopard geckos</a> can wiggle for up to 20 minutes. The tail grows back in most species, but some, like crested geckos, lose their tails for the rest of their lives. <sup about="#mwt928" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-144" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-144"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-144" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 142;" id="mwBWc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBWg">[142]</span></a></sup> In many species the tails are of a separate and dramatically more intense color than the rest of the body so as to encourage potential predators to strike for the tail first. In the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tiliqua_rugosa" title="Tiliqua rugosa" id="mwBWk">shingleback skink</a> and some species of geckos, the tail is short and broad and resembles the head, so that the predators may attack it rather than the more vulnerable front part.<sup about="#mwt932" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-pianka_145-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"pianka"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-pianka-145"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-pianka-145" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 143;" id="mwBWo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBWs">[143]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBWw">Reptiles that are capable of shedding their tails can partially <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Regeneration_(biology)" title="Regeneration (biology)" id="mwBW0">regenerate</a> them over a period of weeks. The new section will however contain cartilage rather than bone, and will never grow to the same length as the original tail. It is often also distinctly discolored compared to the rest of the body and may lack some of the external sculpting features seen in the original tail.<sup about="#mwt937" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-146" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-146"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-146" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 144;" id="mwBW4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBW8">[144]</span></a></sup></p>

</section></section><section data-mw-section-id="34" id="mwBXA"><h2 id="Relations_with_humans">Relations with humans</h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt938" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"further","href":"./Template:Further"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Human uses of reptiles"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBXE"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt938" id="mwBXI">Further information: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Human_uses_of_reptiles" title="Human uses of reptiles">Human uses of reptiles</a></div>

<section data-mw-section-id="35" id="mwBXM"><h3 id="In_cultures_and_religions">In cultures and religions</h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt941" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Reptiles in culture"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwBXQ"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt941" id="mwBXU">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Reptiles_in_culture" title="Reptiles in culture" class="mw-redirect">Reptiles in culture</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwBXY"><a href="./File:Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwBXc"><img resource="./File:Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg/220px-Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="3543" data-file-height="2405" data-file-type="bitmap" height="149" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg/330px-Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg/440px-Laelaps-Charles_Knight-1897.jpg 2x" id="mwBXg"/></a><figcaption id="mwBXk">Painting of fighting "<i id="mwBXo">Laelaps</i>" (now <i id="mwBXs"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dryptosaurus" title="Dryptosaurus" id="mwBXw">Dryptosaurus</a></i>) by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Charles_R._Knight" title="Charles R. Knight" id="mwBX0">Charles R. Knight</a> (1897)</figcaption></figure>
<p id="mwBX4">Dinosaurs have been widely depicted in culture since the English palaeontologist <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen" id="mwBX8">Richard Owen</a> coined the name <i id="mwBYA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur" id="mwBYE">dinosaur</a></i> in 1842. As soon as 1854, the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Crystal_Palace_Dinosaurs" title="Crystal Palace Dinosaurs" id="mwBYI">Crystal Palace Dinosaurs</a> were on display to the public in south London.<sup about="#mwt958" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Torrens_147-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Torrens"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Torrens-147"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Torrens-147" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 145;" id="mwBYM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBYQ">[145]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt962" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Glut_148-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Glut"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Glut-148"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Glut-148" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 146;" id="mwBYU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBYY">[146]</span></a></sup> One dinosaur appeared in literature even earlier, as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens" id="mwBYc">Charles Dickens</a> placed a <i id="mwBYg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Megalosaurus" title="Megalosaurus" id="mwBYk">Megalosaurus</a></i> in the first chapter of his novel <i id="mwBYo"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bleak_House" title="Bleak House" id="mwBYs">Bleak House</a></i> in 1852.<sup about="#mwt947" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-150" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"efn","href":"./Template:Efn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"\n\"Michaelmas term lately over, and the Lord Chancellor sitting in Lincoln&apos;s Inn Hall. Implacable November weather. As much mud in the streets, as if the waters had but newly retired from the face of the earth, and it would not be wonderful to meet a &apos;&apos;Megalosaurus&apos;&apos;, forty feet long or so, waddling like an elephantine lizard up Holborne Hill.\"&lt;ref>{{cite book |last=Dickens |first=Charles J.H. |year=1852 |title=Bleak House |section=[[s:Bleak House/Chapter I|Chapter&amp;nbsp;I: In Chancery]] |page=1 |location=London, UK |publisher=Bradbury &amp; Evans |isbn=978-1-85326-082-7 }}&lt;/ref>\n"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-150" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 3;" data-mw-group="lower-alpha" id="mwBYw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBY0">[lower-alpha 3]</span></a></sup>
The dinosaurs featured in books, films, television programs, artwork, and other media have been used for both education and entertainment. The depictions range from the realistic, as in the television <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Documentary_film" title="Documentary film" id="mwBY4">documentaries</a> of the 1990s and first decade of the 21st century, or the fantastic, as in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Monster_movie" title="Monster movie" id="mwBY8">monster movies</a> of the 1950s and 1960s.<sup about="#mwt966" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Glut_148-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Glut"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Glut-148" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 146;" id="mwBZA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBZE">[146]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt970" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_151-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Gregory"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Gregory-151"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Gregory-151" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 148;" id="mwBZI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBZM">[148]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt974" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Searles_152-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Searles"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Searles-152"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Searles-152" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 149;" id="mwBZQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBZU">[149]</span></a></sup></p>

<p id="mwBZY">The snake or serpent has played a powerful <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Serpent_(symbolism)" title="Serpent (symbolism)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBZc">symbolic role</a> in different cultures. In <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./History_of_Egypt" title="History of Egypt" id="mwBZg">Egyptian history</a>, the Nile cobra adorned the crown of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh" id="mwBZk">pharaoh</a>. It was <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snake_worship" title="Snake worship" id="mwBZo">worshipped</a> as one of the gods and was also used for sinister purposes: murder of an adversary and ritual suicide (<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cleopatra_VII_of_Egypt" title="Cleopatra VII of Egypt" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBZs">Cleopatra</a>). In <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology" id="mwBZw">Greek mythology</a> snakes are associated with deadly antagonists, as a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chthonic" title="Chthonic" id="mwBZ0">chthonic</a> symbol, roughly translated as <i id="mwBZ4">earthbound</i>. The nine-headed <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lernaean_Hydra" title="Lernaean Hydra" id="mwBZ8">Lernaean Hydra</a> that <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hercules" title="Hercules" id="mwBaA">Hercules</a> defeated and the three <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gorgon" title="Gorgon" id="mwBaE">Gorgon</a> sisters are children of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBaI">Gaia</a>, the earth. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Medusa" title="Medusa" id="mwBaM">Medusa</a> was one of the three Gorgon sisters who <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Perseus" title="Perseus" id="mwBaQ">Perseus</a> defeated. Medusa is described as a hideous mortal, with snakes instead of hair and the power to turn men to stone with her gaze. After killing her, Perseus gave her head to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Athena" title="Athena" id="mwBaU">Athena</a> who fixed it to her shield called the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aegis" title="Aegis" id="mwBaY">Aegis</a>. The <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBac">Titans</a> are depicted in art with their legs replaced by bodies of snakes for the same reason: They are children of Gaia, so they are bound to the earth.<sup about="#mwt982" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-BF85_153-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"BF85"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-BF85-153"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-BF85-153" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 150;" id="mwBag"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBak">[150]</span></a></sup> In Hinduism, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snake_worship" title="Snake worship" id="mwBao">snakes are worshipped</a> as gods, with many women pouring milk on snake pits. The cobra is seen on the neck of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Shiva" title="Shiva" id="mwBas">Shiva</a>, while <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Vishnu" title="Vishnu" id="mwBaw">Vishnu</a> is depicted often as sleeping on a seven-headed snake or within the coils of a serpent. There are temples in India solely for cobras sometimes called <i id="mwBa0">Nagraj</i> (King of Snakes), and it is believed that snakes are symbols of fertility. In the annual Hindu festival of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nag_Panchami" title="Nag Panchami" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBa4">Nag Panchami</a>, snakes are venerated and prayed to.<sup about="#mwt986" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Deane_154-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Deane"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Deane-154"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-Deane-154" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 151;" id="mwBa8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBbA">[151]</span></a></sup> In religious terms, the snake and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jaguar" title="Jaguar" id="mwBbE">jaguar</a> are arguably the most important animals in ancient <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica" id="mwBbI">Mesoamerica</a>. "In states of ecstasy, lords dance a serpent dance; great descending snakes adorn and support buildings from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chichen_Itza" title="Chichen Itza" id="mwBbM">Chichen Itza</a> to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tenochtitlan" title="Tenochtitlan" id="mwBbQ">Tenochtitlan</a>, and the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl" id="mwBbU">Nahuatl</a> word <i id="mwBbY">coatl</i> meaning serpent or twin, forms part of primary deities such as <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mixcoatl" title="Mixcoatl" id="mwBbc">Mixcoatl</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Quetzalcoatl" title="Quetzalcoatl" id="mwBbg">Quetzalcoatl</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Coatlicue" title="Coatlicue" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBbk">Coatlicue</a>."<sup about="#mwt990" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-155" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-155"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-155" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 152;" id="mwBbo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBbs">[152]</span></a></sup> In Christianity and Judaism, a serpent appears in Genesis to tempt <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve" id="mwBbw">Adam and Eve</a> with the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Forbidden_fruit" title="Forbidden fruit" id="mwBb0">forbidden fruit</a> from the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tree_of_Knowledge_of_Good_and_Evil" title="Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil" class="mw-redirect" id="mwBb4">Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil</a>.<sup about="#mwt992" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-156" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-156"}}'><a href="./Reptile#cite_note-156" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 153;" id="mwBb8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwBcA">[153]</span></a></sup></p>
